Horiz.</a></span>, 2023, <strong>10</strong>, 5521-5537</div><h1 id="sect224"><span class="title_heading">Nanoarchitected graphene/copper oxide nanoparticles/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> ternary thin films as highly efficient electrodes for aqueous sodium-ion batteries<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a></span></h1><p class="header_text">

      <br><a id="affa"><sup><span class="sup_ref italic">a</span></sup></a><span class="italic">Department of Chemistry, Federal University of Paran (UFPR), CP 19032, 81531-980, Curitiba, PR, Brazil. E-mail: <a href="mailto:aldozarbin@ufpr.br">aldozarbin@ufpr.br</a>;   Tel: +55 41 3361-1565</span>

    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      28th June 2023
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 19th September 2023</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 20th September 2023</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) operating in aqueous electrolyte are an emerging technology that promises to be safer, cheaper, more sustainable and more efficient than their lithium-based counterparts. One of the great challenges associated with this technology is the development of advanced materials with high specific capacity to be used as electrodes. Herein, we describe an ingenious strategy to prepare unprecedented tri-component nanoarchitected thin films with superior performance when applied as anodes in aqueous SIBs. Taking advantage of the broadness and versatility of the liquidliquid interfacial route, three transparent nanocomposite films comprising graphene, molybdenum sulphide and copper oxide nanoparticles have been prepared. The samples were characterized using several techniques, and the results demonstrated that depending on the specific experimental strategy, different nanoarchitectures are achieved, resulting in different and improved properties. An astonishing capacity of 1377 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 0.1 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and a degree of recovery of 100% were observed for the film in which the interactions among the components were optimized. This is among the highest capacity values reported in the literature and demonstrates the potential of these tri-component materials to be used as anodes in aqueous sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><hr><table><tbody><tr><td class="biogPlate"><a href="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-p1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3mh00982c-p1.tif" src="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-p1.gif"></a><p><span class="graphic_title">
                  <span class="bold">Aldo J. G. Zarbin</span>
                </span></p></td><td><i><p>The arrival of Materials Horizons in 2014 brought a new perspective to the Materials Science community, and it has established a reputation for quality and become a source to find the most exciting breakthroughs in the field. I have had the honor and responsibility of being part of the Materials Horizons Advisory Board since 2017, and of having published a review on the technique developed in our group for preparing thin films at liquid interfaces in 2021. We are very happy to contribute this paper for the celebration of a decade of huge success for Materials Horizons. It is time to celebrate its 10th anniversary and to wish it a long life.</p></i></td></tr></tbody></table><hr>
    
      <br><table class="box_border"><tbody><tr><td>
        
          <h2 id="sect300"><span class="a_heading">New concepts</span></h2>
          <span>This work describes unprecedented materials, prepared by an ingenious methodology, with the aim of application in an emergent and promising technology. Tri-component nanoarchitected materials comprising graphene, molybdenum disulfide and copper oxide nanoparticles were prepared and processed as thin and transparent films at the interface between immiscible liquids. This innovative preparation technique allows different experimental arrangements, producing three outstanding samples in which the distribution of the components (or the nanoarchitecture of the films) is different, resulting in different electrochemical properties. The performance of the materials as electrodes in aqueous sodium-ion batteries was evaluated, resulting in an impressive specific capacity due to several factors that are directly dependent on the synthetic pathway, among them: (i) the sum of the capacities of the individual components; (ii) the individual characteristics of each component, and (iii) the synergism resulting from the nanoarchitected structure.</span>
        
      </td></tr></tbody></table><br>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect303"><span class="a_heading">1. Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Novel, unique, and synergistic functionalities arise from materials prepared through the arrangement of different nanostructures in a specific configuration, producing the so-called nanoarchitected nanocomposites.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1</span></sup></a> Based on the chosen constituents, bi-, three- or multi-component materials can be designed, presenting improved mechanical, optical, electrical, chemical and electrochemical properties resulting from the specific interactions among the individual nano-objects.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1,2</span></sup></a> One of the major challenges related to the real application of these nanoarchitected materials concerns their processability, particularly as thin films.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit3"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3</span></sup></a> Traditional techniques for thin film synthesis and/or processing (such as dip-coating,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4</span></sup></a> spin-coating,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit5"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5</span></sup></a> vapor deposition techniques,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6,7</span></sup></a> solgel processes,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8</span></sup></a> hydrothermal routes,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9</span></sup></a> and layer-by-layer deposition,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a> among others<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2</span></sup></a>) are not successful with multicomponent materials prepared in nanoarchitected configurations, and the situation is even worse if transparent and flexible films are desired.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2,11</span></sup></a> Thereby, it is a huge scientific and technological challenge to prepare and process nanoarchitected materials as thin and transparent films that can be deposited over any solid surface or directly integrated into devices in a reliable and reproducible way.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">Our research group developed a straightforward technique for both synthesizing and processing multi-component materials as thin, transparent and homogeneous films.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> The so-called liquidliquid interfacial route (LLIR) is based on the self-assembly of solid materials at the interface between immiscible liquids, resulting in a continuous network that behaves like a malleable film and is easily transferred over any kind of solid substrate.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> One of the many advantages of the LLIR is the possibility of synthesizing compounds in the course of the process, which means that desirable materials can be obtained already processed in a one-pot and single-step procedure. Also, due to the intrinsic characteristics of the LLIR, unprecedented multi-component thin films can be prepared by adjusting the experimental conditions, which could result in different nanoarchitectures. Numerous nanoarchitected thin films have been prepared by us through this technique, based on the mixture of low-dimensional materials, polymers and inorganic compounds,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1315</span></sup></a> and applied in fields such as sensors,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">16,17</span></sup></a> photovoltaics,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18,19</span></sup></a> batteries,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2022</span></sup></a> supercapacitors,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a> and catalysis,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24</span></sup></a> among others.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Energy storage device technology can be positively impacted by nanoarchitected thin films,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25,26</span></sup></a> including novel sustainable, inexpensive and safe devices, for example, aqueous sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Currently, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are a well-established and widely used energy storage device. Nonetheless, this technology presents some drawbacks:<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2729</span></sup></a> traditional electrolytes for LIBs are flammable and volatile solvents;<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> the growth of lithium dendrites during the charge/discharge cycles can connect the electrodes and generate a short circuit;<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3133</span></sup></a> and lithium resources have a limited and poor global distribution.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34,35</span></sup></a> Alternatively, SIBs are emerging as a compelling energy storage device, as they are potentially low-cost, sodium is abundant and widely globally distributed, and sodium mining is accomplished using a cleaner process.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a> Additionally, replacing the flammable and environmentally impactful organic solvents currently used in LIBs with water is a straightforward way towards sustainable and safe devices.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Despite the fact that SIBs work in a similar way to LIBs (which could favor the faster development of SIBs by extrapolating and adapting the mature knowledge gained from LIBs), important differences related to the intercalation/deintercalation of these ions are observed, which makes imperative the development of advanced materials with high specific capacity to be used as electrodes in SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33,38,39</span></sup></a> For aqueous SIBs, the challenge is even greater, as the intercalation/deintercalation process involves the hydrated cation and the electrochemical window of the aqueous electrolyte is narrow.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40,41</span></sup></a> Electrode materials based on different nanostructures have been recently prepared for this specific purpose.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4245</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">The goal of this work is to demonstrate the potential of the LLIR to produce unique tri-component thin-film nanoarchitected materials to be used as high-capacity electrodes in aqueous SIBs. By combining molybdenum disulfide (MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>), copper oxide nanoparticles (Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O-NPs) and graphene, synergistic and improved properties arising from the specific nanoarchitecture are obtained, and the resulting thin-film material presents one of the highest specific capacities ever reported for this kind of application. MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is a bidimensional (2D) material with a large interlayer distance (0.62 nm)<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46,47</span></sup></a> and pseudocapacitive properties, which allows surface redox reaction and the reversible intercalation/deintercalation of different ions.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit48"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4850</span></sup></a> The theoretical specific capacity of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is 669 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit51"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5153</span></sup></a> but even higher values can be reached by controlling its morphology or preparing MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-based nanocomposites.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45,54,55</span></sup></a> The volume changes of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> during ion intercalation/deintercalation (which causes mechanical stress, re-stacking and structure degradation),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35,50</span></sup></a> as well as its inherently low electronic conductivity,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit48"><sup><span class="sup_ref">48,49</span></sup></a> are characteristics that decrease the experimental capacity of this material, and which can be improved by preparing suitable nanocomposites.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit56"><sup><span class="sup_ref">56</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Copper oxides (Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>O and CuO) have attracted great attention as electrodes in metal-ion batteries due to their theoretical specific capacity, modular morphology, easy production and low toxicity.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit57"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5767</span></sup></a> CuO exhibits a higher theoretical specific capacity (674 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) compared to Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (369 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>), but interestingly, some properties of the mixture of these two copper oxides, such as electrochemical reactivity and electrical/ionic conductivity, have been demonstrated to be higher than those of the individual components.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit68"><sup><span class="sup_ref">68,69</span></sup></a> However, Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/CuO presents several drawbacks for use as electrodes in SIBs, including significant volume changes during conversion reactions (over 228%),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit70"><sup><span class="sup_ref">70,71</span></sup></a> low energy density, low power density, and poor stability.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit72"><sup><span class="sup_ref">72,73</span></sup></a> Once again, the preparation of nanocomposites with suitable materials, such as graphene, is one way to overcome these limitations.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45,56,74,75</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Graphene is a promising material for use as electrodes in metal-ion batteries. The structure of graphene, which is characterized by a one-atom-thick bidimensional material of sp<small><sup>2</sup></small>-hybridized carbon atoms (the stacking of which forms graphite)<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit76"><sup><span class="sup_ref">7678</span></sup></a> can theoretically host metal ions on both sides of the sheet, resulting in a theoretical capacity twice that of graphite (744 <span class="italic">vs.</span> 372 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit78"><sup><span class="sup_ref">78</span></sup></a> Thus, preparing nanocomposites comprising graphene, MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles could result in a material in which the three components are expected to contribute to the electrode capacity, and the interactions among them can result in synergistic properties to further increase this capacity.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">We recently demonstrated the preparation of thin and transparent bi-component films between MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/carbon nanotubes and graphene/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles through the LLIR.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit56"><sup><span class="sup_ref">56,75</span></sup></a> Herein, unprecedented tri-component nanoarchitected materials combining MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, graphene and Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles are introduced. The huge potential of the LLIR to prepare these complexes and multi-component materials is demonstrated for three different samples based on three different experimental approaches, which results in different nanoarchitectures and different properties. Finally, the use of these films as anodes in aqueous SIBs is demonstrated, and the results are among the highest performances ever reported for this kind of application.</p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect383"><span class="a_heading">2. Methodology</span></h2>
      
        
        <h3 id="sect386"><span class="b_heading">2.1. Chemicals</span></h3>
        <span>Ammonium molybdate ((NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>6</sub></small>Mo<small><sub>7</sub></small>O<small><sub>24</sub></small>4H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, Vetec), ammonium sulfide ((NH<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>S, solution 20%, Vetec), sulfuric acid (H<small><sub>2</sub></small>SO<small><sub>4</sub></small>, 98%, Anidrol), acetonitrile (gradient grade for liquid chromatography, 99.9% LiChrosolv, Merck), graphite (90%, Graflake, Nacional de Grafite), potassium permanganate (KMnO<small><sub>4</sub></small>, synth, 99%), hydrochloric acid (HCl, 37% Panreac), sodium nitrate (NaNO<small><sub>3</sub></small>, Vetec), hydrogen peroxide aqueous solution (H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, 30%, Vetec), toluene (99.9% Sigma Aldrich or 99% Neon), copper nitrate (Cu(NO<small><sub>3</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>, Vetec), sodium borohydride (NaBH<small><sub>4</sub></small>, 98%, Acros Organic), and sodium chloride (NaCl, Neon) were used as received. The solutions and dispersions were prepared with deionized water using a Milli-Q ultra-pure water purification system with <span class="italic">R</span> = 18.2 M cm.</span>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect407"><span class="b_heading">2.2. Synthesis, exfoliation and film preparation of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small></span></h3>
        <span>MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> was synthesized and exfoliated according our previous report.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit79"><sup><span class="sup_ref">79</span></sup></a> The thin films of neat MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> were prepared through the LLIR according the procedure summarized in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a</a>:<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit79"><sup><span class="sup_ref">79</span></sup></a> 5 mL of an acetonitrile dispersion of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (0.14 mg mL<small><sup>1</sup></small>) was added to a 50 mL round-bottom flask and maintained under magnetic stirring at 2500 rpm. Then, 20 mL of ultrapure water and 20 mL of toluene were added to the system, and the resulting biphasic system was magnetically stirred for 12 h. After interruption of the stirring, a homogeneous, continuous and self-assembled film of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> was obtained at the liquidliquid interface. The film was transferred to a glass Becker containing the substrate (glass, quartz, Si, FTO/glass electrodes) at the bottom and deposited by pulling the substrates across the liquid/liquid interface. The film was dried at 200 C for 4 h before characterization.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3mh00982c-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">Schematic representation of the general steps for the thin film preparation: (a) MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>; (b) rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O or rGO; (c) rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL; (d) rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing; (e) rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect439"><span class="b_heading">2.3. Preparation of the graphene/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles thin films</span></h3>
        <span>The graphene/copper oxide nanoparticles thin films were prepared according a methodology previously reported by us, as summarized in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit75"><sup><span class="sup_ref">75</span></sup></a> The method involves the simultaneous reduction of graphene oxide (GO) and Cu<small><sup>2+</sup></small> in a water/toluene biphasic system in air. GO was prepared using a modified Hummers method.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12</span></sup></a> Then, 8 mL of a 0.3 mg mL<small><sup>1</sup></small> Cu(NO<small><sub>3</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small> aqueous solution, 4 mL of an aqueous dispersion of GO (0.15 mg mL<small><sup>1</sup></small>) and 18 mL of water were mixed in a 50 mL round bottom flask, resulting in a total volume of 30 mL for the aqueous phase. Afterwards, 20 mL of toluene was added to the flask to obtain the liquidliquid interface. The entire system was stirred for two hours at 1500 rpm. After this time, 3 mL of an aqueous NaBH<small><sub>4</sub></small> solution (40 mg mL<small><sup>1</sup></small>) were added under stirring, and the reaction was performed for 30 minutes. Subsequently, the stirring was turned off, and a dark gray thin film was observed at the liquidliquid interface, which was characterized by reduced graphene oxide/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles (hereafter referred as rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O thin film). The aqueous phase was exchanged for fresh ultra-pure water (by removing the aqueous phase using a Pasteur pipette and adding a novel portion of 30 mL of water), and the system was stirred for a few minutes. This procedure was done to remove any soluble impurities remaining in the system, and it was repeated 10 times. Subsequently, the same procedure was carried out with toluene. As a control, the same procedure was also performed without copper nitrate, aiming for the preparation of a neat rGO film. Both films were deposited over suitable substrates as described earlier in Section 2.2.</span>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect459"><span class="b_heading">2.4. Thin films of nanocomposites comprising rGO, Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small></span></h3>
        <span>Starting from the LLIR, three different approaches were adopted to prepare the tri-component films, as described in the following sections and schematically represented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c, d and e</a>. The strategies will be referred to here as the layer-by-layer (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a>), mixing (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a>) and <span class="italic">in situ</span> synthesized (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1e</a>) strategies.</span>
        <div>
          
          <span id="sect471"><span class="c_heading_indent">2.4.1. rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanocomposite prepared by the layer-by-layer (LbL) strategy. </span>
          <span>This is the simplest approach, in which the previously prepared rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> films at the watertoluene interface were sequentially deposited over the substrates in a layer-by-layer-like process (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a>). A film of rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O prepared exactly as described earlier (Section 2.3) was deposited over suitable substrates and dried at 200 C for 4 hours. Subsequently, a film of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> prepared as described in the Section 2.1 was deposited over this rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O film, followed by another drying process at 200 C for 4 hours. For comparison, the same procedure was carried out to produce a rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> bi-component film (by depositing a MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> film over a previously deposited rGO one). The films obtained in this way will be referred to here with the suffix  LbL (from layer-by-layer): rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL.</span>
        </span></div>
        <div>
          
          <span id="sect493"><span class="c_heading_indent">2.4.2. rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanocomposite prepared by film mixing. </span>
          <span>The second approach was based on a mixture of two films that were already stabilized at liquidliquid interfaces (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a>). Each film was individually prepared (MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O, prepared as described in Sections 2.2 and 2.3) and kept at the watertoluene interface. Afterwards, 10 mL of the toluene and 15 mL of the water of each water/film/toluene system were removed using a pipette. The toluene/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-film/water system was magnetically stirred at 2500 rpm, and the toluene/rGO-Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O-film/water system was added over it. The mixture was kept under magnetic stirring for 12 hours, and after this, the film at the liquidliquid interface was transferred to substrates and dried according to the procedure described earlier. A control sample starting from a neat rGO film (instead of a GO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O film) was also prepared. The films will be referred to here with the suffix mixing: rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing.</span>
        </span></div>
        <div>
          
          <span id="sect513"><span class="c_heading_indent">2.4.3. rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanocomposite chemically synthesized <span class="italic">in situ</span>. </span>
          <span>The last nanocomposite was chemically synthesized <span class="italic">in situ</span>, directly at the liquidliquid interface, by performing the same reaction described in Section 2.3 to produce the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O films, but with MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> dispersed in one of the liquid phases (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1e</a>). First, 8 mL of an aqueous solution of Cu(NO<small><sub>3</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small> (0.3 mg mL<small><sup>1</sup></small>) was mixed with 4 mL of an aqueous dispersion of GO (0.15 mg mL<small><sup>1</sup></small>), 7 mL of a 0.14 mg mL<small><sup>1</sup></small> acetonitrile solution of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and 11 mL of water in a 50 mL round bottom flask, resulting in 30 mL of aqueous phase. Afterwards, 20 mL of toluene was added to create the liquidliquid interface, and this system was kept under magnetic stirring for two hours at 1500 rpm. After that time, 3 mL of an aqueous solution of NaBH<small><sub>4</sub></small> (40 mg mL<small><sup>1</sup></small>) was added with a syringe. After 30 minutes, the stirring was interrupted, and a grey film was observed at the interface between the two liquids. The cleaning process and deposition over substrates were carried out as described earlier. A film synthesized in the same way but without Cu(NO)<small><sub>3</sub></small> was prepared, aiming to obtain a rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> bi-component film. The films will be referred to here with the suffix  <span class="italic">in situ</span>: rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span>.</span>
        </span></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect543"><span class="b_heading">2.5. Characterization</span></h3>
        <span>X-ray diffraction (XRD) was performed using a Shimadzu diffractometer (XRD-6000) with CuK radiation (<span class="italic"></span> = 1.5418 ) and a thin-film accessory. Raman spectra were acquired using a confocal Raman spectrometer (WITec Alpha 300R) with a 532 nm laser line at 0.9 mW of power, with 30 accumulations of 10 s of acquisition time. UV-Vis spectra were obtained using a Shimadzu UV-2450 spectrophotometer directly on the films deposited on quartz substrates, using air as a reference. The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images were obtained using a Mira FEG-SEM (Tescan) with an accelerating voltage of 10 kV coupled to an EDS detector (Oxford Instruments) for elemental analysis. For the transmission electronic microscopy (TEM) images, the films were deposited over copper grids covered with a hollow carbon thin film, and the images were collected using a JEOL 120 kV instrument at 100 kV. High-resolution transmission electronic microscopy (HRTEM) images were acquired using a JEOL JEM F200 device with a resolution of 0.1 nm, and the EDS-type elemental chemical analysis was obtained using an SDD detector with an area of 100 mm<small><sup>2</sup></small> in a scanning mode (STEM). Electrochemical measurements were performed with an Autolab potentiostat operated <span class="italic">via</span> GPES and NOVA 1.11 software, using a conventional three-electrode cell with a Pt wire as a counter electrode, Ag/AgCl (3.0 mol L<small><sup>1</sup></small>) as the reference electrode and the thin films deposited over ITO as the working electrode. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was performed from 0.2 V to 0.4 V at different scan rates (5 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small> to 50 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>) in an aqueous solution of 0.1 mol L<small><sup>1</sup></small> NaCl. The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) measurements were performed using NaCl aqueous solution (0.1 mol L<small><sup>1</sup></small>) as the electrolyte in the range of 0.2 V to 0.4 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Ag/AgCl) at five current densities (0.1, 0.25, 0.5, 0.75, 1 and 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>), and evaluation of the materials for 200 or 1000 consecutive cycles was performed at a current density of 2 A g<small><sup>1</sup></small>. The cyclic voltammograms were integrated to obtain the approximate electroactive mass of the components in each film: MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (9.35  10<small><sup>8</sup></small> g cm<small><sup>2</sup></small>), rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O (3.41  10<small><sup>7</sup></small> g cm<small><sup>2</sup></small>), rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL (5.19  10<small><sup>8</sup></small> g cm<small><sup>2</sup></small>), rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL (2.89  10<small><sup>7</sup></small> g cm<small><sup>2</sup></small>), rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing (5.07  10<small><sup>8</sup></small> g cm<small><sup>2</sup></small>), rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing (1.58  10<small><sup>7</sup></small> g cm<small><sup>2</sup></small>), rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> (5.39  10<small><sup>8</sup></small> g cm<small><sup>2</sup></small>) and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> (2.06  10<small><sup>7</sup></small> g cm<small><sup>2</sup></small>). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed in 0.1 mol L<small><sup>1</sup></small> aqueous NaCl solution at 0.1 V with an amplitude of 10 mV and frequency of 10 MHz to 10 kHz. Electrochemical quartz crystal microbalance (EQCM) measurements were performed using an Autolab 128n in EQCM mode and the software nova 2.15, with a gold circular electrode (3 mm radius) coupled to a quartz crystal, a Pt wire as a counter electrode, and Ag/AgCl (3.0 mol L<small><sup>1</sup></small>) as the reference electrode, in 0.1 mol L<small><sup>1</sup></small> aqueous solution of NaCl in the range of 1.0 to 0.6 V at 10 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>.</span>
      
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect595"><span class="a_heading">3. Results</span></h2>
      <span>The main challenge presented in this work was to obtain the unprecedented tri-component materials directly processed as transparent films, and to demonstrate their efficiency as electrodes in aqueous SIBs. Beyond describing a nanoarchitected material that had never been reported before, this work also demonstrates the potential and broadness of the LLIR to achieve these sophisticated materials in different nanoarchitectures, which will result in different properties, as will be demonstrated in the following.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">Nine different films have been prepared, consisting of three control samples (MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, rGO, and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O), along with six nanocomposites generated through three different approaches. The first strategy involved producing the films separately and subsequently depositing the most conductive film (rGO or rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O) onto the substrate, followed by the deposition of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> film above the previous one, in a layer-by-layer (LbL) method (samples rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL). The second method involved the preparation of each individual film at a liquidliquid interface, followed by the combination (mixing) of those films at the liquidliquid interface before deposition over the substrates, resulting in the samples rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing. Finally, the third method involved the chemical synthesis of the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O <span class="italic">in-situ</span> at the liquidliquid interface in the presence of a MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> dispersion in a one-step approach, resulting in the rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> films.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Digital photographs of the films deposited over planar quartz substrates are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>. The high homogeneity, transparency, and optical quality of the films are evident. The MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> film has a characteristic orange-yellowish hue, while the rGO and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O films appear gray. Due to the multilayer deposition method, darker films were obtained for the layer-by-layer films, compared to the those synthesized using the mixing and <span class="italic">in situ</span> strategies.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3mh00982c-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">Digital images of the films deposited over glass (a); UV-Vis (b) and Raman (c) (<span class="italic"></span> = 532 nm) spectra of the different nanocomposites.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">
        <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a> and Fig. S1a (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) present the UV-Vis spectra of the samples deposited over planar quartz substrates. The main absorption bands are detailed in Table S1 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> spectrum (Fig. S1a, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) presents the characteristic A and B bands at 673 nm and 621 nm, respectively, due to excitonic transitions in the valence band related to excitons at point K of the Brillouin zone, and the bands C and D at 453 nm and 392 nm due to the excitonic transitions from the valence to the conduction band of the semiconductor.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit80"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8082</span></sup></a> The spectrum of rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O (Fig. S1a, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) shows a single band at 270 nm attributed to the * transition in graphene,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit75"><sup><span class="sup_ref">75,83</span></sup></a> confirming the reduction of GO to rGO in this sample. This band is also observed in the rGO sample at 259 nm. It is well known that the energy of the * transition in rGO samples is dependent on the effectiveness of the GO to rGO reduction (which is directly related to the sp<small><sup>2</sup></small> backbone restoration), and occurs at lower energy for the most-reduced samples.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit84"><sup><span class="sup_ref">84,85</span></sup></a> The occurrence of this band at 259 nm for rGO and 270 nm for rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O indicates that the GO to rGO reduction was more effective in the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O sample compared to in the neat rGO. Furthermore, another band at 305 nm can be seen in the neat rGO spectrum (Fig. S1, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) due to the n* transitions involving residual oxygenated functional groups that were not fully removed during the reaction, which provides further evidence that the GO to rGO reduction was not as effective in the neat rGO reaction as in the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O one.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit86"><sup><span class="sup_ref">86</span></sup></a> Taking into account the fact that the amounts of GO and NaBH<small><sub>4</sub></small> are exactly the same in both reactions, as well as the fact that the reducing agent was shared with the Cu<small><sup>2+</sup></small> cations during the synthesis of rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O (meaning less reducing agent available to GO), we would expect the opposite. This apparent paradox can be explained by considering that the nascent Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles catalyze the GO to rGO reduction, promoting more-reduced rGO after 30 min of reaction. The difference in the energy of the UV-Vis band was not observed for the same reactions conducted for 24 h (data not shown), corroborating this hypothesis. No bands due to the Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles were detected in the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O spectrum.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">The spectra of the nanocomposites (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a>) show all the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and graphene bands described earlier, corroborating the presence of both components in the samples. Some shifts in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> bands were observed in the spectra of the <span class="italic">in situ</span> synthesized samples (Table S1, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), mainly in the band-gap-related C and D bands, which may be indicative of changes in the electronic structure of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> due to the interactions among the components.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">The Raman spectra of the films are presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2c</a> and Fig. S1b, S2 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), and a summary of the main features is presented in Table S2 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> film (Fig. S1b, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) presents two characteristic bands at 384 and 412 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> due to the E<small><sup>1</sup></small><small><sub>2g</sub></small> and A<small><sub>1g</sub></small> modes, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit87"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8792</span></sup></a> The graphene bands are readily observable in the rGO and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O samples:<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit93"><sup><span class="sup_ref">93100</span></sup></a> the G band at 1588 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> (due to the E<small><sub>2g</sub></small> mode); the D band at 1352 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> (resulting from a symmetry break due to structural defects or partially disordered graphitic domains); the D band at 1620 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> (also due to disorder or defects in the structure, usually observed in well-reduced rGO samples in which the sp<small><sup>2</sup></small> structure was efficiently rebuilt after the reduction process); the 2D band at 2683 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> (attributed to the structural organization of graphene in the two-dimensional plane); and the D + G band at 2940 cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> (a combinatory mode). Comparing the rGO and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O spectra, there is a slight shift in the band positions (Table S2, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), the bands are narrower in the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O spectrum, and the D band does not appear in the rGO spectrum. All these features are spectral evidence that the rGO is more reduced in the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O than in the rGO films,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit87"><sup><span class="sup_ref">87,9597</span></sup></a> corroborating the data obtained from UV-Vis spectroscopy.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">All the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and graphene bands discussed earlier can be found in the spectra of the nanocomposites presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2c</a> and Fig. S2 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), including the evidence for more-reduced rGO in the samples containing the Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles. The ratio between the intensities of the D and G band (the <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small> ratio) was calculated (Table S2, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), and a significant increase in <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small> was observed for all the Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O-containing materials, as expected for more effectively reduced rGO.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit92"><sup><span class="sup_ref">92,98</span></sup></a> The chemical interactions among the components can also be responsible for the increase in the <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small> ratio observed for the nanocomposite samples.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit94"><sup><span class="sup_ref">94,99101</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Fig. S3a (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) shows the X-ray diffraction patterns of the thin films. MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and all the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-containing films present an intense peak due to the (002) planes of 2H-MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> at 6.15  (JCPDS 371492), which is enlarged in comparison to that of bulk MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, evidencing the presence of few-layer MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit102"><sup><span class="sup_ref">102</span></sup></a> The other two less-intense peaks of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> expected to be seen in this range (specifically, the (100) and (103) peaks) are not observable in the patterns presented in Fig. S3a (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) due to the specificity of the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> sample used in this work (characterized as few-layer, polycrystalline and exhibiting a small grain size of 64 nm  42 nm)<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit79"><sup><span class="sup_ref">79</span></sup></a> and due to the data being collected directly from the thin films using a grazing angle accessory. A large and low-intensity signal at 3.85  can be seen in the profile of the rGO and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O films due to the irregular stacking of the rGO sheets as a result of their re-organization in the solid state after the reduction process.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit103"><sup><span class="sup_ref">103</span></sup></a> The copper oxide nanoparticle signal appears between 25 and 45 (in 2<span class="italic"></span>). An expanded view of this region can be seen in Fig. S3b (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), demonstrating that the samples are constituted of a mixture of CuO and Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>O.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit75"><sup><span class="sup_ref">75</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">In order to identify the nature of the copper oxide produced in the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> tri-component sample, several syntheses were carried out, and the film was collected from the liquidliquid interface with the aim of accumulating material to acquire a conventional X-ray diffractogram profile. The result is shown in Fig. S3c (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>); the material is characterized by the presence of the (002) peak of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> at <span class="italic">d</span> = 6.15  (JCPDS 371492); the broader and poorly defined (002) peak of the rGO at 3.97 ; three peaks attributed to Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>O at 3.01  (110), 2.46  (111) and 2.13  (200) (JCPDS 74-1230); and four peaks due to CuO at 2.75  (110), 2.52  (111), 2.32  (200) and 1.87  (202) (JCPDS 72-0629). It is interesting to note that although the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> samples were prepared in exactly the same way (except, of course, that the latter was prepared in the presence of dispersed MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>), the former has predominantly CuO and the latter a mixture of Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (predominant) and CuO. These findings suggest that MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is not just a spectator during the synthetic procedure, and someway affects the reaction pathway.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">
        <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3ai</a> (and Fig. S4S8, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) show the scanning electron microscopy images of the films. The MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> thin film (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a> and Fig. S4ac, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) is composed of a continuous network of small platelets (average size of 64 nm  42 nm),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit56"><sup><span class="sup_ref">56,79</span></sup></a> while rGO (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a> and Fig. S4df, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) consists of large interconnected graphene sheets (1 m  500 nm) with clear edges and folds.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit93"><sup><span class="sup_ref">93</span></sup></a> Notably, this rGO morphology can also be seen in the sample rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a> and Fig. S4gi, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), together with cubic-shaped Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit75"><sup><span class="sup_ref">75</span></sup></a> (average lateral size of 120 nm) distributed over, between and wrapped by the graphene sheets. These three morphological profiles appear on different nanoarchitectures in the bi- and tri-component nanocomposite films: (i) in both rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a> and Fig. S5ac, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3g</a> and Fig. S5df, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), the morphology observed is consistent with films that have been sequentially deposited, one on top of another, in which the morphology of individual films can be detected; (ii) in contrast to the LbL samples, both the rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a> and Fig. S6ac, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3h</a> and S6de) films exhibit closer interaction among the components. The MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> plates are sandwiched between graphene sheets, creating a more intimate contact between the materials; (iii) a similar nanoarchitecture was observed in both the rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3f</a> and Fig. S7ac, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3i</a> and Fig. S7df, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) films, which were characterized by very close and intimate contact among the three components, but the Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O NPs were larger in size (approximately 200 nm), demonstrating that the presence of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> during the synthesis also affects the final particle size. Images using a backscattered electron (BSE) detector were collected for the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> sample (Fig. S8, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). In the BSE images, the regions containing heavy elements (high <span class="italic">Z</span> values) appear brighter, facilitating the identification of the large amount and good distribution of copper oxide nanoparticles and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> nanoplatelets.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3mh00982c-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">Scanning electron microscopy images of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (a); rGO (b); rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O (c); rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL (d); rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing (e); rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> (f); rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL (g); rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing (h); and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> (i). Energy dispersive spectroscopy data (jp) of rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span>, including point analysis (jl) and mapping (mp).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">Energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) analysis (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3jp</a> and Fig. S9, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) provided information on the elemental composition of the materials. The survey spectra of all the nanocomposite films (Fig. S9, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) show the presence of C, Mo, and S (the Si signal is from the substrate). In addition, Cu and O were also detected in the samples containing Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles, as expected. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3jl</a> present the spatially resolved spectra, and <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3mp</a> show the elemental mapping obtained for the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> sample. Analysis of these data revealed that the cubic particles of this sample are composed of Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/CuO, as shown by the intense Cu and O signals in the point analysis spectra of these cubes (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3l</a>) and by the Cu mapping (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3n</a>). The MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> plates were found to be homogeneously distributed throughout the sample (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3o</a>).</p>
      <p class="otherpara">
        <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4</a> and Fig. S10S16 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) show the transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images collected for the samples rGO, MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O, rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span>. Graphene (Fig. S10, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) has large, flat sheets with small layers, as well as some characteristic folds. On the other hand, MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (Fig. S11, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) presents a very small two-dimensional plate (64 nm  42 nm, as previously shown by SEM), structure with the stacking of a few layers (3 to 10 layers, Fig. S12, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). The rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> sample (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4ab</a> and Fig. S13, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) has MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> plates homogeneously distributed across large graphene sheets. The rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4cf</a> and Fig. S14, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) sample, in addition to the cubic morphology seen <span class="italic">via</span> SEM in regions with large amounts of particles, also shows very small (10 nm) and mostly spherical particles distributed throughout the graphene sheets. The presence of CuO (with a crystallographic plane <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>200</sub></small> = 0.32 nm) and Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (with a crystallographic plane <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>111</sub></small> = 0.24 nm) in rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O was confirmed through high-resolution images and FFT (fast Fourier transform) analysis, as shown in Fig. S15 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). EDS analysis obtained using STEM (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4gj</a>) of the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O sample show Cu and O on the darker and geometric-shape particles in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4g</a>, again corroborating the Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O composition of those nanoparticles, and a homogeneous distribution of C matching the graphene-like morphology.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3mh00982c-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">Transmission electron microscopy images of the samples rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (a) and (b) and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O (c)(f). Energy dispersive spectroscopy mapping of rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O (g)(j).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The TEM and HRTEM images of the sample rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5</a> and Fig. S16, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) reveal details regarding the nanoarchitected way in which the three components are organized. As can be seen, the film is supported by interconnected graphene sheets on which the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and the Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O NPs are homogeneously distributed (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a, b</a> and Fig. S16, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>). Looking closer, as in the two different regions illustrated in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5c and d</a>, it can be noted that the three components are in very intimate contact with no segregation or predominance of individual components. This finding highlights the hypothesis that graphene and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> can act as nucleation and growth sites for the Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles. Looking even closer (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5e and f</a>), it can be seen that in addition to the cubic-shaped ones, the sample is full of very small Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles (1 to 10 nm) spread over the entire film. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5f</a> elucidates the intimate contact between these small Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles, the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (the fringes of the few-layer MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> can be detected mixed with the Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O NPs) and the graphene.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3mh00982c-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">Transmission electron microscopy images (a)(g) and energy dispersive spectroscopy mapping images (h)(j) of the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> sample.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The EDS elemental mapping (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5hj</a>) of the region shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5g</a> clearly shows the distribution of the elements and corroborates the identification of each component in the film.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">The electrochemical performance of the nanocomposites deposited over ITO/glass was investigated using cyclic voltammetry (CV), galvanostatic charge/discharge curves (GCD), an electrochemical quartz crystal microbalance (EQCM) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), carried out in NaCl aqueous solution as electrolyte. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6</a> shows the CVs of the nanocomposites and the control samples at 5 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small> within the potential range of 0.2 V to 0.4 V in an aqueous solution of 0.1 mol L<small><sup>1</sup></small> NaCl. Fig. S17 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) shows the CV data at different scan speeds (5 to 50 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>). Pseudo-capacitive behavior was observed in the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> thin-film (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6a</a>), with a redox pair at an <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>1/2</sub></small> of 0.14 V. Two mechanisms are predicted in the literature to explain this profile:<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit104"><sup><span class="sup_ref">104106</span></sup></a> the first one describes the pseudo-capacitive behavior from the faradaic process in which Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions are intercalated into the lamellar structure of MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (<a title="" href="#eqn1">eqn (1)</a>), and the second one is related to the non-faradaic process due to the formation of an electrical double layer due to the adsorption of cations onto the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> surface.</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1"><span id="eqn1">MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> + <span class="italic">x</span>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + <span class="italic">x</span>e<small><sup></sup></small>  Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (<span class="italic">x</span> &lt; 1)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3mh00982c-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2023/MH/d3mh00982c/d3mh00982c-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">Cyclic voltammograms of the thin films: (a) MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>; (b) rGO; (c) rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O; (d) rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL; (e) rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing; (f) rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span>; (g) rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL; (h) rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing; (i) rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span>. Electrolyte: 0.1 mol L<small><sup>1</sup></small> NaCl aqueous solution. Scan speed of 50 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The CV profile of rGO is typical of a capacitive material related to the occurrence of an electric double-layer, as can be seen in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6b</a>. The nanocomposites between rGO and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> exhibit similar behavior, showing a faradaic process at <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>1/2</sub></small> = 0.1 V (attributed to MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>) and a rectangular profile coherent with the rGO. The faradaic process is more defined in the CV curve of rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing and occurs at lower potential than in the other samples (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>1/2</sub></small> = 0.06 V) due to the improved contact between the two components, as described earlier.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">The cyclic voltammogram of rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O exhibits two well-defined redox pairs, a predominant one at <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>1/2</sub></small> = 0.19 V (0.26 V/0.12 V), corresponding to the Cu<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Cu<small><sup>2+</sup></small> process, and a less-intense one at 0.16 V/0.05 V, attributed to the Cu<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Cu<small><sup>2+</sup></small> oxidation pair of small copper oxide nanoparticles with different species/sizes/shapes in the film. As previously described, this sample contains two species of copper oxide, with CuO being present in greater quantity and Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>O in smaller quantity. These species have different sizes and shapes that are consistent with the processes observed in the film. Furthermore, CV experiments were conducted on rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O in different electrolytes (aqueous solutions of NaCl, NaNO<small><sub>3</sub></small>, and Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>SO<small><sub>4</sub></small>), and the CV profile obtained in NaNO<small><sub>3</sub></small> (Fig. S18, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) indicates several well-defined oxidation processes that are consistent with the presence of Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>O/CuO nanoparticles. Literature reports of these materials mainly focus on organic electrolytes that are not within our working range. Other systems have been studied in alkaline aqueous electrolytes, and, although the processes may be slightly different, they occur in the same potential range as our work, and these studies have previously demonstrated the Cu<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Cu<small><sup>2+</sup></small> process in this range.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit107"><sup><span class="sup_ref">107109</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">The tri-component materials present significant differences in their CV profiles due to the different ways in which they are nanoarchitected. The rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-LbL sample shows an oxidation process at 0.07 V attributed to MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> and another redox pair at 0.2 V/0.09 V attributed to Cu<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Cu<small><sup>2+</sup></small> processes; the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing film exhibits a single redox process at <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>1/2</sub></small> = 0.11 V (0.17 V/0.05 V), which has a profile similar to that of the rGO/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-mixing control sample. This process is attributed to the combined processes of both materials (MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O) due to their close contact, as discussed earlier. The rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> sample exhibits two processes: a redox pair at 0.17 V/0.06 V and another at 0.23 V/0.08 V. These processes are comparable to those observed in the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O control sample and can be attributed to the Cu<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Cu<small><sup>2+</sup></small> redox process, although the profiles are shifted in comparison to the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O control sample due to the larger amount of Cu<small><sub>2</sub></small>O relative to CuO in this sample. Also, the redox process attributed to MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (<a title="" href="#eqn1">eqn (1)</a>) at the same potential should contribute to the observed intensity of the redox pair. The oxidation process becomes noticeably broader as the scanning speed of the cyclic voltammograms increases (Fig. S17I, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), indicating the occurrence of two processes in the same region, possibly originating from Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O nanoparticles and MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Fig. S19 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) displays the Nyquist plots derived from the EIS data of all the nanocomposite films. The rGO film closely resembles an ideal capacitor according to its curve profile and CV measurements; when the system kinetics are straightforward, current limitation occurs due to mass transfer phenomena, resulting in a more-linear and less-distinct semicircular region that corresponds to the sodium diffusion process within the electrode.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit110"><sup><span class="sup_ref">110112</span></sup></a> In contrast, the neat MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> is characterized by an evident semi-circle at high frequency, corresponding to the charge transfer resistance, indicating that the electrochemical process is governed by charge transfer phenomena between the active material and the electrolyte.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit111"><sup><span class="sup_ref">111</span></sup></a> The diameter of the semi-circle is directly proportional to the charge transfer resistance and inversely proportional to the rate of heterogeneous charge transfer. The incorporation of Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O NPs or MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> into graphene to give the bi-component material leads to the appearance of the semi-circle in the high-frequency region, and the tri-component materials exhibit smaller semi-circle diameter. Notably, the rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> film possesses the lowest charge transfer resistance, which implies enhanced conductivity.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit113"><sup><span class="sup_ref">113</span></sup></a> This is consistent with the superior specific capacity results of these samples, as will be discussed further.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">The electrochemical quartz crystal microbalance (EQCM) is a convenient tool for monitoring mass changes during the electrochemical evaluation of electrode materials. It provides direct information that allows the understanding of various surface processes related to sensors, capacitors, batteries, and other electrochemical devices.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit114"><sup><span class="sup_ref">114117</span></sup></a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7</a> and Fig. S20 (ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) show the CV profiles recorded in NaCl 0.1 mol L<small><sup>1</sup></small> and the respective EQCM frequency responses recorded simultaneously for the samples MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>, rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O and rGO/Cu<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>O/MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>-<span class="italic">in situ</span> deposited on a gold/quartz electrode. The slight variations observed in the voltametric behavior of these films compared to those presented earlier are due to the working electrode utilized (films deposited over gold/quartz) as well as to the wider potential range selected, which was determined by EQCM stabilization (a feat that was unattainable within a smaller range). In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7a</a>, the CV obtained for MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> reveals two redox pairs with <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>1/2</sub></small> values of 0.24 V and 0.25 V, respectively, which are ascribed to the pseudocapacitive processes discussed earlier. Thomas <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit115"><sup><span class="sup_ref">115</span></sup></a> presented the electrodeposition technique for obtaining MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> using EQCM under different working conditions than those used in this study, but observed a similar electrochemical profile.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit115"><sup><span class="sup_ref">115</span></sup></a> As shown in the EQCM results for MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7a</a>), a slight increase in mass occurs near a potential of 0.2 V (1) during the reduction process, followed by a significant increase starting at 0.3 V (2). During oxidation, the mass begins to decrease at around 0.3 V (3), and another drop is observed at 0.2 V (4). The suggested mechanism, which is schematically represented in <a title="" href="#eqn1">eqn (1)</a>, is based on Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> cation intercalation during the reduction of the electrode material, which involves the filling of the electrode with cations, leading to a mass increase. Conversely, cation deintercalation occurs during the oxidation process when Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> cations are removed from the electrode material, resulting in a decrease in mass.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit116"><sup><span class="sup_ref">116</span></sup></a> This is exactly what can be seen in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7a</a> for the MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> film: the mass variations happen in the same region as the charge changes (redox processes), suggesting an intercalation/deintercalation process within the material. Notably, the deintercalation at 0.2 V (4) is in close proximity to the discharge potential, as discussed in the subsequent GCD analyses.</p>
